{"uri": "8119877884", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "22:35:20", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T22:35:20Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T22:35:03Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.6823529601097107, "url": "https://gazette.com/ap/sports/for-jim-curtin-cavan-sullivan-signing-with-the-union-is-a-full-circle-moment/article_63cd3b06-85c6-59a8-8670-c19532dd4a88.html", "title": "For Jim Curtin, Cavan Sullivan signing with the Union is a 'full-circle moment'", "body": "PHILADELPHIA -- Union manager Jim Curtin doesn't get emotional in public very often. Maybe when one of his players scores a big goal, but that's about it.\n\nOn Thursday, though, Curtin was nearly moved to tears over one aspect of Cavan Sullivan's arrival to the big time.\n\nA quarter-century ago, Cavan's grandfather Larry was Curtin's college coach at Villanova. In Curtin's senior season, Cavan's father Brendan joined the Wildcats' coaching staff.\n\nNow Curtin is coaching the 14-year-old who might become the best ever from a family that he called \"kind of Philadelphia soccer royalty.\"\n\n\"There's an element of debt, excitement, a kind of full-circle moment,\" Curtin said at a news conference in Cavan's honor Thursday at Subaru Park. The precocious attacking midfielder sat next to him on stage, and much of the Sullivan family -- Brendan, mother Heike, and fellow sons Quinn, Ronan and Declan -- sat in the front row of the seats.\n\nCurtin praised Larry as \"a guy that taught me so much about the game, about being a leader a person, and a good soccer player as well,\" and called Brendan \"the first guy that I saw that was a professional-level player, that I saw day-in and day-out.\"\n\nThough MLS's launch in 1996 was a year before Curtin started at Villanova, American players were still hard to find. The sport certainly wasn't like what it is now, where there are countless players and fans can watch every big league worldwide.\n\nSullivan's signing with the Union is a symbol of that, too. For as much as everyone wanted him to start his pro career at the hometown club, the Union still had to make an offer worth picking over starting at England's Manchester City, Germany's Borussia Dortmund, or other big European clubs that wanted him.\n\nNew details of the deal\n\nIn the end, Sullivan will get the best of both worlds. He'll play for the Union until he's 18, then move to Man City for a pre-agreed price of around $5 million.\n\nAnd he'll get paid plenty before then. Sullivan signed the largest homegrown player contract in Major League Soccer history, multiple sources told The Philadelphia Inquirer, with the league breaking its homegrown player salary cap -- around $200,000 a year -- to get the deal done.\n\nIt will be just over $500,000 per season, one source with knowledge of the matter said, surpassing a special benchmark in league history: the salary that Freddy Adu, American soccer's first 14-year-old phenom, earned as a rookie with D.C. United 20 years ago.\n\nMLS wasn't ready for a player of his age back then, and Adu endured a tumultuous career that included 20 months with the Union from 2011-13.\n\nThe landscape is far better now, and the Union are a big reason why. Their youth academy includes a full-time high school for top prospects that Cavan attends, that his oldest brother Quinn -- now a Union first-team veteran -- attended before, and that Brendan works at as a history teacher.\n\nFeatured Local Savings\n\nOn top of that, the Union have sent some of those players on to big international stages. Brenden and Paxten Aaronson, Mark McKenzie and Auston Trusty all grew up here, play in major European leagues, and have been with the senior U.S. national team.\n\n\"We have a track record now,\" academy director Jon Scheer said. \"We have a history of developing players within our academy, within MLS, and transitioning them on to bigger clubs in the global game ... I think we now have a proof of concept with our competitive strategy that families and players can truly believe in.\"\n\nPatience with playing him\n\nOf course, lots of people in town want to know when Sullivan will play for the Union's first team. Curtin preached patience, but also didn't hide from Sullivan's immense potential.\n\n\"The game will tell us,\" he said. \"He's going to work hard and get on the field on merit first and foremost. But I'll just say publicly, it's a lot closer than people realize. Our team, whatever we need that week, he's going to be called upon quickly, because he deserves it.\"\n\nThe odds are that if we're going to see Sullivan with the first team this year, it will be during the late-summer Leagues Cup tournament instead of the regular season. The Union's group stage opener is July 27, and if Sullivan plays in that game he'll break Adu's record for MLS's youngest ever player by two days.\n\nSullivan will definitely play for the Union's reserve team before then, and probably often. Coach Marlon LeBlanc has known Sullivan since he was eight years old, and Sullivan has already played for the reserves as an amateur a few times. In his debut on March 24, he came off the bench and created the game-winning assist.\n\n\"My job is to put him in a position to be successful,\" LeBlanc said. Everybody wants to see Cavan out there on Saturday night [when the first team plays], but Cavan understands the process to get to Saturday night. We're going to make sure that he's learning, he's progressing, and he's held accountable as well.\"\n\nBut they'll also let him express himself on the field.\n\n\"We're not going to stifle his creativity,\" LeBlanc said. \"We're going to give him a platform to do it, but we're going to make sure that he does it the right way. And I know Cav, and he's going to do it the right way -- and he doesn't want it any other way, and that's one of the best things about working with Cavan.\"\n\nIt matters a lot to LeBlanc, and the organization as a whole, that Sullivan should have the time and space to be a kid.\n\n\"He's good enough to play and be there, but I want to make sure that people remember that he's 14 years old,\" he said. \"That will be a big part of his development: him getting to stay in his home, in his bed, in his house, with his family, is only going to speed that part up too. And he gets to do this at his own timeline, his own process.\"\n\n\u00a92024 The Philadelphia Inquirer. Visit inquirer.com. {"uri": "8119325520", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "14:39:13", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T14:39:13Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T14:38:49Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.6196078658103943, "url": "https://www.inquirer.com/soccer/cavan-sullivan-debut-union-soccer-academy-manchester-city-20240509.html", "title": "Is Cavan Sullivan really that good? Here's what to know about the Union academy and its teen phenom.", "body": "Cavan Sullivan is one of the biggest names Philly sports fans might not know (yet), but they should definitely pay attention to the 14-year-old soccer prodigy.\n\nHis home club, the Philadelphia Union, put pen to paper on a deal that secures Sullivan's future stays in his hometown for at least three seasons before an eventual departure to English Premier League giant Manchester City. The Union and Man City brokered a deal that will introduce Sullivan to European soccer when he turns 18 -- a waiting period due to work visa restrictions for minors.\n\nPhilly can be proud because if there was ever a living embodiment of the term \"homegrown,\" Sullivan is its latest descriptor.\n\nSo who is this kid? Where did he come from and why should you care? Inquirer soccer writers Kerith Gabriel and Jonathan Tannenwald bring you up to speed.\n\nIt was almost impossible for Sullivan not to rise to the pedigree he already had before his 16th birthday. His grandfather, Larry, is Northeast Philly soccer royalty, having starred at the now-defunct North Catholic High School in the 1960s, before playing at Temple. But Sullivan is known for his coaching prowess, serving as the head coach at St. Joseph's, Father Judge High School, and Camden Catholic High, which preceded his 17-year stint as the head coach at Villanova.\n\nCavan's dad, Brendan, was also a player. He is now a teacher at YSC Academy and runs a Conshohocken-based soccer school that provides intrinsic coaching and development.\n\n\"I was thrilled with Cav's decision to decide to start his career here, play with his brother, drive in every day with his brother, live in the house for a couple more years,\" he told The Inquirer. \"19124, 19137, those were our zip codes [in the city]. We were bought in through birthright, for better or for worse.\"\n\n\" READ MORE: Why Cavan Sullivan, hyped as the world's best 14-year-old soccer player, chose to turn pro with the Union\n\nCavan's uncle is Chris Albright, a Philly-area native who was a member of the U.S. men's national team and played for multiple Major League Soccer teams including the Union, where he later became the general manager/technical director for four seasons before departing for the same job at FC Cincinnati.\n\nAnd his brother, Quinn Sullivan, is a current midfielder on the Union, making these two the second brother duo to play for the Union since Medford, N.J., natives, Brendan and Paxten Aaronson. -- KG\n\nIt's unknown when he'll get his debut with the first team, but all signs point to the start of MLS's upstart Leagues Cup tournament next month, which would provide an opportunity for even more exposure than games during the regular season. The Leagues Cup, which pits MLS clubs and teams from Mexico's Liga MX together for a chance to gain entrance into the larger Concacaf Champions Cup, was already a draw for fans but gained immense popularity after it became the introduction to Lionel Messi playing on American soil with Inter Miami FC.\n\nThe Leagues Cup being an ancillary offshoot of the MLS regular season also allows key players to get rest while providing an opportunity -- at least in the early stages -- to players who might not get sizable minutes during the season. In short, exposure and a need for a more versatile roster make this tournament the perfect stage to introduce the wunderkind. -- KG\n\n\" READ MORE: 'Cavan is special': Union's Ernst Tanner says Cavan Sullivan is one of the best he's ever seen\n\nHere's how I'd put it, having watched him play for the Union's reserves and youth teams. He's 14 years old, and we just can't forget that at any point along the way here. There's nothing new about teenage prospects who don't live up to the hype, just as there's nothing new about teenage prospects who do.\n\nHaving said that, for what he is right now, yeah, he's really good. You don't just walk into your first reserve team game as a substitute, when you're clearly younger than everyone else on the field, and put the game-winning assist on a dime 25 minutes later.\n\nThe best part of what Sullivan gains from turning pro with the Union is time. He'll play here, but the Union won't rush him or try to burden him with too many expectations. He'll get to live at home with his family, grow up, then show off when he's ready. And when he is, we'll all see it. -- JT\n\nThe Union run their own youth soccer teams for kids as young as eight years old, partly to grow the game and partly to look for the Philly area's best prospects. Things get serious at the under-12 age level, when the Union start picking the cream of the crop. From then on, the Union's teams play against other MLS clubs' teams, and other major youth teams across the region and nation.\n\nThe under-15 and under-17 teams are really big-time. They play against not just American clubs, but international clubs on foreign tours and in big tournaments like the Generation adidas Cup, which the Union's under-17s have won two years in a row.\n\nNow here's the kicker (sorry). In 2013, the Union launched their own full-time high school for their very best prospects, called YSC Academy. A lot of the famous homegrown players you've seen at Subaru Park over the years -- Brenden and Paxten Aaronson, Quinn and Cavan Sullivan, Jack McGlynn, Mark McKenzie, and more -- came through the school.\n\nThose players all turned pro straight out of the school, and in some cases even before graduating. Their classmates who didn't turn pro right away went to college, in places as big as the Big Ten, ACC, and even the Ivy League. And the way MLS rules work, even though the league has a college draft, those kids are exempt from it and the Union keep their rights.\n\nThe Union's academy is so good at this that players from other parts of the country now want to join it. McGlynn came from New York City, and David Vazquez came from Los Angeles. -- JT\n\n\" READ MORE: Inside the Union's controversial recruitment of top prospect David Vazquez\n\nYes, really. College soccer players still make it to MLS, whether or not they came through teams' youth academies, and the league has a draft for them. But college sports aren't really a thing in the rest of the world like they are here.\n\nBig teams in Europe, South America, and Africa develop their players in their own youth academies, and that's it. It took MLS a while to get on board with doing it that way, but they have, and they've gotten good at it. Honestly, it's the best thing the league does, even if Lionel Messi gets all the attention.\n\nImagine something like this happening in basketball: the 76ers running their own AAU squad and a high school, developing those kids, and keeping their pro-rights even if they go to Villanova or Temple for a few years. There wouldn't have been a \"Villanova Knicks\" situation like there was this ... ah, it's too soon, isn't it. -- JT\n\n\" READ MORE: Complaints about Villanova hyping up Knicks alumni are 'idiotic,' Josh Hart says\n\nMoney. And in the case of Cavan Sullivan, potentially millions. Unlike America's pay-to-play model when it comes to youth sports, specifically in the case of travel soccer, in the academy -- while parents still pay -- the idea is that a top MLS academy team like the Union's is developing talent desirable to top clubs in Europe and elsewhere, where teams have the cash to splash at the ready for young talent.\n\nNot only does the team get paid when one of their players leaves to sign a big European contract, but they can potentially get a cut of every future deal he signs, making players like Sullivan valuable beyond what he can contribute while wearing a {"uri": "8119720205", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "19:58:43", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T19:58:43Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T19:56:53Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5568627715110779, "url": "https://www.delcotimes.com/2024/05/09/when-might-we-see-cavan-sullivan-play-for-the-union-the-game-will-tell-us/", "title": "When might we see Cavan Sullivan play for the Union? 'The game will tell us'", "body": "CHESTER -- A few dates in Cavan Sullivan's soccer trajectory are known: His signing Thursday as the fifth-youngest in MLS history, his eventual departure to Manchester City when he turns 18 in 2027.\n\nBut when Sullivan's first MLS minutes with the Philadelphia Union will occur is much less certain. The consensus at his introductory press conference was something approximating soon, maybe.\n\nThat is as much expectations management as it is reality meeting hype, not dissimilar to the collision of a 14-year-old and a grown man twice his age.\n\nHere's how Union head coach Jim Curtin, notoriously reluctant to be drawn on such things, put it:\n\n\"The game will tell us,\" Curtin said. \"He's going to be training with the first team, and that's how you're going to get better. ... That is how development happens and gets accelerated quickly. So he's going to jump into training. We're certainly not afraid to put him on the field at any time.\"\n\nExpectations are stratospheric for Sullivan, who at age 14 has a professional contract and the interest of the presiding power in the English Premier League. Sullivan has played up in age for most of his time in the Union Academy, which he joined at 10. He was a star at the U-15 level at age 13, at the U-17 level by 14 and has made two appearances for Union II, already with an assist.\n\nThe burning question, particularly for a Union side looking in need of a spark in an 0-2-1 run, is when Sullivan might play. But the time horizons that Curtin and sporting director Ernst Tanner hypothesized skew more towards years than months.\n\nThis weekend's visit from Orlando City is definitively out, not that it was ever in. Tanner said Sullivan hasn't trained this week while managing a \"small overload injury.\" He should return to Union II training next week and build up to training with the first team.\n\nThe Union's investment is over the long-term - the outlay of the largest Homegrown contract in MLS history not only makes them custodian to his development but guarantees a transfer fee when (and it no longer seems an if) he goes to Europe. They won't rush either his development as a player or a teenager.\n\nFor the moment, both processes are in the hands of Curtin and Union II coach Marlon LeBlanc. Both have long histories with young players, and both are parents of teenagers. (Kellen LeBlanc is an attacking midfielder a year younger than Sullivan and another promising Academy talent.)\n\n\"Cavan is 14,\" Curtin said. \"His job here will be to be a kid and play and reach his full potential, and our job here is to create an environment where he can thrive in.\"\n\nThere's one crucial difference in the two coaches' approaches. Curtin's job is contingent on winning games in MLS. LeBlanc's in MLS Next Pro is not; it's merely a nice if not necessary byproduct of the talent he has that Union II is currently second in its conference.\n\nThat offers the runway to get Sullivan minutes for his benefit, even in situations where he might not be the player who gives Union II the best chance to win the day, as is Curtin's unflinching rubric.\n\n\"My role is going to be to allow him to go out and fail and succeed,\" LeBlanc said. \"He'll be learning in front of the fans on gameday. You guys will get to see that.\"\n\nThe first priority is handling the individual. Second is to get him used to playing kids older by several years with Union II. And then, that will put him in position to contribute to the first team, which Curtin doles out strictly on merit.\n\n\"This game will tell us when the next move comes,\" Curtin said. \"That could be in a year. That could be in two years. That could be in three years, whatever it might be. He's going to play at the very highest level that this sport has. Right now, we think this is the best environment for him to play.\"\n\n\"It's something different than U-17 tournaments, even an international one, by physical standards and opposition,\" Tanner said. \"So that's what we need to see now, how quick he adapts there and gets over the level. And the next step will certainly be that he trains with the first team, and then we take it from there.\n\n\"I'm not a seer. {"eng-9553314": {"articles": {"results": [{"uri": "8119869967", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "22:26:13", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T22:26:13Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T22:25:41Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.6823529601097107, "url": "https://www.delcotimes.com/2024/05/09/union-sign-14-year-old-generational-talent-cavan-sullivan-to-largest-mls-homegrown-deal/", "title": "Union sign 14-year-old 'generational talent' Cavan Sullivan to largest MLS Homegrown deal", "body": "CHESTER -- With an age difference of 5\u00bd years, Cavan Sullivan didn't give much thought to sharing a soccer field that wasn't his Philadelphia backyard with older brother Quinn.\n\nCavan's growth would offer plenty of options not to do so. Hyped in some corners as the best 14-year-old soccer player in the world, Cavan has already drawn interest for years from a who's who of international clubs.\n\nBut when it came time to choose the first steps of what he hopes is a long professional journey, Sullivan wanted to stay close to home and to family.\n\n\"I've just been in and around the club since I was a little kid,\" Sullivan said Thursday at an introductory press conference at Subaru Park upon signing with the Union. \"So this is my home. And it's an honor to sign here for my first professional contract. It means a lot that they see the talent in me at such a young age. I want to prove myself and work for this club, for this city and for all these fans.\"\n\nSullivan's signing makes history, something that all involved hope becomes common in the career of a \"generational talent.\" The midfielder is 14 years, 224 days old, the fifth-youngest player to sign an MLS contract. Should he debut before the end of July, he'd be the youngest player in MLS (and American professional team sports) history. His contract is the richest Homegrown deal ever. It comes with a reported agreement that he will join English giants Manchester City when he turns 18.\n\nBefore all that, though, is a chance to ply his trade daily with his big brother. Cavan, born in 2009, is the youngest of four brothers. Quinn, a Union Homegrown signee in 2021 and born in 2004, has become an MLS starter and youth national team fixture. In between are 2007 birth year twins Ronan and Declan.\n\nNow Cavan and Quinn will share an office, essentially, long before either can legally buy a beer.\n\n\"I didn't think that, especially this early, it'd be occurring, but I'm super happy that it is and super excited for it,\" Quinn said. \"Definitely would be hoping for it later down the line, as with all my brothers - Ronan and Declan still have the opportunity to do that - and hopefully, I'll be able to share the field with all of them at some point.\"\n\nFamily was part of the pull for Cavan. Union coach Jim Curtin played at Villanova under Cavan's grandfather, Larry Sullivan, one of the doyens of Philadelphia soccer whose influence on Curtin choked the coach up Thursday. Brendan Sullivan, the boys' father, was an assistant at Villanova and now teaches at YSC Academy. He and wife Heike played soccer at Penn.\n\nThe only limiting factor on Cavan's next steps were legal. Union sporting director Ernst Tanner said discussions were held before Sullivan's 14th birthday last September, the earliest he could have been employed. Labor laws are tricky, given that he would work in Pennsylvania for a league headquartered in New York and incorporated in Delaware. Sullivan cannot be transferred to another country until his 18th birthday, though with a German passport, he can join a foreign academy at age 16. The Union wouldn't have received a transfer fee for his services in that case. Thursday's deal allows them to recoup the cost of his development and reap the benefits of his on-field production until he's ready for Europe.\n\nSullivan's hype is considerable. Tanner called him, \"certainly among the top three talents I have seen the year range so far.\" Curtin used the terms \"relentless\" and \"fearless\" in describing his skills. They echo a consensus from Sullivan's play with the Union Academy and youth national teams.\n\n\"He's a specialist in that when he gets the ball, it's electric,\" Curtin said. \"Something's going to happen here. He's going to do something that I don't think of as a coach, that I can't teach as a coach. He's going to do something outside the box. And that's something that you can't really put words to.\"\n\n\"He's got this attacking mindset that is fearless,\" Union II coach Marlon LeBlanc said. \"He creates goals, he scores goals, he can do it in every facet. He's an absolutely fantastic young footballer, and you throw (age) 14 in there and that's why we're all sitting here in this room having this discussion.\"\n\nSullivan has, from his earliest days at FC Delco and the Union Academy, played up in age. He helped the Union made the Generation Adidas Cup final in the U-15 bracket at age 13. This spring, at 14, he powered a U-17 title. Shortly thereafter, he made his debut with Union II.\n\nCurtin used Thursday's press conference to reinforce the club's value of youth development. For a club that aspires to be the gold standard in developing young Americans, Sullivan's decision to stay home is a significant coup. It's validation that the club can help him improve, with a support system that will help tackle the physical and mental challenges along the way.\n\nThat includes a big brother to both help him and let him know gently when he needs to help himself.\n\n\"I'm happy to be in the locker with him and help him in whatever direction he needs,\" Quinn Sullivan said of his little brother. \"And it's great to have someone in the locker room that has that experience already, four years in, lead them in the right direction and let him do his thing as well. There's a fine line between helping out and doing too much, and I'll make sure not to go over that line.\"\n\nCavan chose his words carefully, flanked by people who both praised him voluminously while vowing to keep him humble. In doing so, he showed some of the mentality that was being so lavishly lauded.\n\n\"To be honest, nothing really has happened yet,\" Cavan said. \"The work starts now. I've got a lot to do. My main goal was to win MLS Cup. Development starts now, and it does feel nice, but still a lot of work to do for my part and a lot of room to grow.\"", "source": {"uri": "delcotimes.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "The Delaware
